  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_df586f21-ccbf-4ba5-ad1b-dc8989682867'
      });

  <span id="freeText11122590629618814453" style="display:none">Möbius, ein umworbener weil genialer Physiker, will eine gefährliche Verstrickung von Wissenschaft und Politik verhindern und täuscht darum vor, geisteskrank zu sein, damit er in einer Klinik verschwinden und die gewünschte Kooperation (oder Kollaboration) mit der Macht boykottieren kann. Aber der geschützte Raum ist nicht dicht: Zwei Grossmächte haben Agenten in die Klinik eingeschleust, auch diese täuschen Krankheiten vor. Der Fluchtort ist zur Falle geworden.<br /><br />This a previously-published edition of ISBN 3257230478</span>

<div id="review_1005724213" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1005724213" />
    <a title="Lisa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa"><img alt="Lisa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480492067p2/32532774.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1005724213?book_show_action=true">Jul 24, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa">Lisa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=drama">drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=unforgettable">unforgettable</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1005724213" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11993667378327295845">Imagine an insane person with an abnormal need to dominate others at any cost. Imagine that person having access to nuclear power. <br/><br/>Imagine the feeble resistance of scientists, thinking that they can escape from true insanity (and responsibility for creating weapons of total destruction) by hiding in an institution for the mentally ill. Imagine the despair when they realise that they are trapped in a system running like a fine-tuned machine to the tune of the Pied Piper, relying on human rats to </span>
  <span id="freeText11993667378327295845" style="display:none">Imagine an insane person with an abnormal need to dominate others at any cost. Imagine that person having access to nuclear power. <br /><br />Imagine the feeble resistance of scientists, thinking that they can escape from true insanity (and responsibility for creating weapons of total destruction) by hiding in an institution for the mentally ill. Imagine the despair when they realise that they are trapped in a system running like a fine-tuned machine to the tune of the Pied Piper, relying on human rats to follow the powerful psychopath and to turn the notions of good and evil, sane and insane, right and wrong, true and false, knowledge and belief into their complete opposites.<br /><br />Dürrenmatt wrote an inventive masterpiece of universal impact, signalling to the world that civilisation is but a thin layer of polish, and that the bullying power of ruthless narcissists will almost always be a step ahead of reflective thinking and responsible behaviour. If we needed proof that brutal ego-centrism is more successful than the much-cited values of "common sense" and "solidarity", The Physicists offers it in an uncomfortably accurate drama. <br /><br />But it also shows us that art and sciences have an ethical core, a message to deliver to those living under the spell of an insanely needy personality. We might not be able to stop those who are violent enough to disregard all of humanity, but as long as art and science exist, we can speak up in the name of reason. Historical mistakes can't be undone, so we have a universal duty to keep thinking creatively to deal with them.<br /><br />This timeless classic is probably my favourite Dürrenmatt - an absolute must-read!</span>
  <a data-text-id="11993667378327295845" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2265261828" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13760247032397676468"><b>Macavity’s Not There</b><br/><br/>In T.S. Eliot’s poem, <i>Macavity: The Mystery Cat</i>, the protagonist is an accomplished feline criminal who causes havoc and confusion but is always is out of sight when the sheriff arrives: <i>“... when a crime’s discovered, Macavity’s not there.”</i> In Durrenmatt’s play, Macavity <u>is</u> there, in a Swiss asylum, but he disappears into a psychotic mind, or rather three such minds, of the men identified as Newton, Einstein, and Möbius. Three nurses have been strangled - by those who appare</span>
  <span id="freeText13760247032397676468" style="display:none"><b>Macavity’s Not There</b><br /><br />In T.S. Eliot’s poem, <i>Macavity: The Mystery Cat</i>, the protagonist is an accomplished feline criminal who causes havoc and confusion but is always is out of sight when the sheriff arrives: <i>“... when a crime’s discovered, Macavity’s not there.”</i> In Durrenmatt’s play, Macavity <u>is</u> there, in a Swiss asylum, but he disappears into a psychotic mind, or rather three such minds, of the men identified as Newton, Einstein, and Möbius. Three nurses have been strangled - by those who apparently love them - with only the delusional husks of the perpetrators left behind. Like Macavity, these are <i>“outwardly respectable”</i> but really quite dangerous.<br /><br />While all are mad, each is mad in a different way. Newton only pretends to be Newton; he is really, he believes, Einstein. The pretence is a courtesy to the one who believes he’s Einstein. Or perhaps the pretence is a pretence and he really believes he is Newton, or someone else. Möbius has visions and conversations with King Solomon. Einstein is a homicidal maniac. But <i>Fraulein Doktor</i> is in charge of everything, including identity: <i>“It is I who decide who my patients think they are.”</i><br /><br />A bit like Chesterton’s <i>A Man Called Thursday</i>, things are not what they seem. As an intimate and devotee of Carl Jung, <i>Fraulein Doktor</i>, is part of a symbolic subterfuge. The name of the place, <i>Les Cerisiers</i>, points to Chekov’s <i>The Cherry Orchard</i> as an inspiration. High intrigue, depth psychology, and murder mystery, all in the space of a theatrical hour. A piece hard to better for complex simplicity. To echo Eliot, <i>“There never was a Cat of such deceitfulness and suavity.”</i><br /><br />Postscript: <i>The Physicists</i> addresses a similar ethical theme to Julien Benda’s classic, <i>The Treason of the Intellectuals </i>but with a rather different twist. It also might be considered a sort of riposte to Hermann Hesse’s <i>The Glass Bead Game</i> regarding the desirability of a ‘disengaged’ <i>intelligentsia</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13760247032397676468" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_3655534499" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3655534499" />
    <a title="Greta" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/111566858-greta"><img alt="Greta" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586844759p2/111566858.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3655534499?book_show_action=true">Nov 23, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Greta" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Greta" href="/user/show/111566858-greta">Greta</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" 9780394172460" href="/book/show/1990794.The_Physicists">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/111566858-greta?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/111566858-greta?shelf=best-reviews">best-reviews</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3655534499" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9956780095075516798"><b> Physicists gone mad </b><br/><br/>„The Physicists“ is a satiric play by Friedrich Dürrenmatt, written in 1961. Three patients in a mental institution, who seem to believe that they are famous physicists, murder their nurses. The play is extremely smart, funny and political - dealing with questions of scientific ethics and intellectual responsibilities, particularly in the context of the Cold War.<br/><br/><b> Newton - Einstein - Möbius </b><br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1606183212i/30420027._SX540_.jpg' width='500' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><i>The three mental patients "Einstein"- Möbius - "Newton" and the insane chief psychiatr</i></span>
  <span id="freeText9956780095075516798" style="display:none"><b> Physicists gone mad </b><br><br>„The Physicists“ is a satiric play by Friedrich Dürrenmatt, written in 1961. Three patients in a mental institution, who seem to believe that they are famous physicists, murder their nurses. The play is extremely smart, funny and political - dealing with questions of scientific ethics and intellectual responsibilities, particularly in the context of the Cold War.<br><br><b> Newton - Einstein - Möbius </b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1606183212i/30420027._SX540_.jpg" width="500" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><i>The three mental patients "Einstein"- Möbius - "Newton" and the insane chief psychiatrist von Zahnd</i><br><br>At the core of the story are three physicists, who pretend to be insane, which is why they live in a mental institution, where the story is set. The first patient, Ernst Heinrich Ernesti, claims to be "Albert Einstein", the second one ,Herbert Georg Beutler, says he‘s "Isaac Newton" and the third one is named Johann Willhelm Möbius and states that he is frequently visited by the biblical son Salomon. Möbius says that he has discovered the world formula, which could destroy all humankind, which is why he pretends to be psychotic to make himself seem incompetent and prevent the abuse of his discovery. „Newton“ and „Einstein“ only pretend to be insane as well and are actually agents send by secret services. Their mission was to be committed to the mental institution to observe Möbius and get his secret discoveries.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1606182620i/30420004._SX540_.jpg" width="500" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><i>Einstein and friends become very naughty by strangling all of their nurses, after they fell in love with them</i><br><br>When the play begins, "Einstein" has just killed one of his nurses, and the police are examining the scene. This was the second slaying of a nurse, the first having been committed by "Newton“ and the third murder of a nurse follows quickly when Möbius strangles a nurse as well. The three physicists murdered their nurses because they feared for their secret to be discovered. Möbius destroys his formula, when the police starts to investigate and convinces the other two to keep silent as well. But their agreement comes too late, as Mathilde von Zahnd, the chief psychiatrist and owner of the mental institution, had already discovered and copied Möbius discoveries. She turns out to be the only one who is actually insane and actually believes that she is acting on behalf of King Salomon and attempts to gain world domination with Möbius world formula. But because the physicists are now publicly stigmatized as insane due to their engineered murders, they have to stay in the mental institution and are not able to prevent von Zahnd‘s plans.<br><br><b> Interpretation </b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1606182620i/30420005._SX540_.jpg" width="500" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><i>Möbius what have you done? - oh no, not again </i><br><br>The play raises questions of scientific ethics and claims that what has once been thought can never be taken back. Thereby "Newton" (Beutler) and "Einstein" (Ernesti) represent two different models of science : One stands for the „pure science“ which only discovers for its own sake, and the other one represents the pragmatic approach of applied science. Both models lead to knowledge, which ultimately fails and is deadly. To solve this dilemma, Möbius chooses to isolate himself but he ultimately fails as well, which leads to the conclusion that science inevitably leads to negative consequences. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1606182620i/30420006._SX540_.jpg" width="500" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><i>Möbius gets in trouble after realizing, that "Einstein" and "Newton" are in fact secret agents to steel his formula </i><br><br>Because the chief doctor von Zahnd is the one who is truly insane, it remains unclear, whether she is actually a doctor, or just a patient in the clinic as well, who only pretends to be a doctor. Her insanity is obvious and she is ultimately convinced that King Salomon appears to her and that she has to take over the world domination. Whether "Newton" and "Einstein" are just insane, agents, or insane agents, remains unclear as well, but this question is only secondary. More important is the realization, that scientific discoveries always find their way into the wrong hands. Additionally the satire states that this problem can only be solved by people working together. Möbius attempted solution to isolate himself wasn’t effective, as von Zahnd outsmarted him. The three physicists can‘t escape their dilemma, as they remain passively trapped in the institution. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1606183870i/30420033._SX540_.jpg" width="500" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><i>Of course their is never time for the detective to interrogate the murderers - mental patients are very sensitive and need to recover first.</i><br><br>Furthermore it‘s extremely funny that the story constantly makes fun of the nature and concept of mental institutions, completely questioning their effectiveness. When the investigator comes to the sanatorium to interrogate "Einstein" for murdering his nurse, he naturally can‘t speak to the patient because "Einstein" is busy calming down by playing the violin. Because he is a sensitive mental patient, those very important and therapeutic sessions can‘t be interrupted and of course the chief doctor von Zahnd isn‘t available for questioning as well, because she has to join him. Of course this situation repeats itself with every physicist and murder. The murdered nurses were completely delusional as well and always fell in love with the physicist, wanted to marry them, claimed they weren’t insane but geniuses and tried to free them, which made them become a threat and which is why they had to die.<br><br><b>Context</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1606182202i/30419994._SX540_.jpg" width="500" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><i>Atom bomb "Little boy" - Hiroshima 06.08.1945</i><br><br>The context of the story is the political situation in the late 1950s and early 1960s. It was inspired by WWII and the Cold War between the United States and the Sowjet Union. The race for nuclear power started with the drop of the atom bombs on Hiroshima and Nagasaki and now the fear of a nuclear war was forced ,when the Berlin Wall was build in 1961. Already four years before the play was written, 18 German nuclear scientists had formed the „Göttinger Eigtheen“ that turned against the military use of nuclear power and against equipping the army with nuclear weapons.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1606184938i/30420070._SX540_.jpg" width="500" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><i>Albert Einstein - Nobel Price 1921. His scientific discoveries didn't directly contribute to the building of the atom bomb</i><br><br>Albert Einstein was known to be a pacifist, but signed a letter to US President Franklin D. Roosevelt in 1939 urging him to develop the atomic bomb. Einstein and other qualified scientists expressed their concerns, that Nazi Germany could use nuclear energy to build an extremely powerful bomb of a new type, that could destroy entire ports. The letter contributed to the installation of the Manhattan Project, which developed the atomic bombs dropped on Hiroshima and Nagasaki in 1945, that killed around 200.000 people. While Einstein later said he felt he had no choice but to encourage the US to develop the technology, he called the letter his „one great mistake“, having learned that Germany was never close to developing atomic bombs. <br><br><i>“Reality can be only partially attacked by logic.” </i><br><br>„The Physicists“ is simply genius! A very intelligent, educational, provocative and extremely funny satire!</span>
  <a data-text-id="9956780095075516798" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_751064546" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/751064546" />
    <a title="Warwick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53165-warwick"><img alt="Warwick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1510406793p2/53165.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/751064546?book_show_action=true">Oct 27, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Warwick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Warwick" href="/user/show/53165-warwick">Warwick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780802144270" href="/book/show/6693479-the-physicists">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Manny</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53165-warwick?shelf=physics">physics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53165-warwick?shelf=swiss-lit">swiss-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53165-warwick?shelf=drama">drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53165-warwick?shelf=switzerland">switzerland</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer751064546" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17114753378866164414"><br/><blockquote>
  <i>The fact is, there's nothing more scandalous than a miracle in the realm of science.</i>
</blockquote><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1383074545i/6374948.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Three of history's greatest physicists meet in a drawing-room: Newton, Einstein and Möbius. Newton has a bottle of cognac hidden in the fireplace. Einstein has just strangled a woman to death. And Möbius is being visited by the ghost of King Solomon, who is telling him the secrets of a Unified Field Theory.<br/><br/>Except the drawing-room belongs to a Swiss insane asylum, and the three men are patients.<br/><br/>What follows is</span>
  <span id="freeText17114753378866164414" style="display:none"><br><blockquote>
  <i>The fact is, there's nothing more scandalous than a miracle in the realm of science.</i>
</blockquote><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1383074545i/6374948.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Three of history's greatest physicists meet in a drawing-room: Newton, Einstein and Möbius. Newton has a bottle of cognac hidden in the fireplace. Einstein has just strangled a woman to death. And Möbius is being visited by the ghost of King Solomon, who is telling him the secrets of a Unified Field Theory.<br><br>Except the drawing-room belongs to a Swiss insane asylum, and the three men are patients.<br><br>What follows is a playful mash-up of a country-house murder-mystery with a scientific drama-of-ideas. At first the execution reminded me of Tom Stoppard – high praise round my way, because I think Stoppard's one of the greatest writers alive. But while Stoppard's work is always discursive, and never tries to convince you of a particular position, Dürrenmatt takes a more polemic approach here – especially in the second act, where the characters are increasingly fixated on the dangers of scientific discoveries falling into the wrong hands.<br><br><blockquote>She considered me an unrecognized genius. She didn't realize that today it is the duty of a genius to remain unrecognized.</blockquote><br><br>Great line. Of course when this was first performed in 1962, the Cold War was still on and this felt more of a live issue. It was less than 20 years since the real Einstein had famously said that if he'd known what the results of nuclear research would be, he would have become a watchmaker. (He also said, ‘The discovery of nuclear chain reactions need not bring about the destruction of mankind any more than did the discovery of matches,’ but no one remembers that one.)<br><br>These are still crucial questions, but I think the sophistication of the debate has slightly overtaken the moral of this play. Nevertheless, there is a huge amount of fun and intellectual enjoyment to be had here, with jokes and theories and interesting dramatic ideas on every page. I'd love to see it staged – but if waiting for your local theatre to get on board seems daunting, the ideas involved make this well worth reading in the meantime.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17114753378866164414" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2426969988" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2426969988" />
    <a title="David Schaafsma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma"><img alt="David Schaafsma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574997804p2/269235.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2426969988?book_show_action=true">Jun 17, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Schaafsma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Schaafsma" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma">David Schaafsma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780573013409" href="/book/show/73469.The_Physicists">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=drama">drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=science">science</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2426969988" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5181729719100482334">"The content of physics is the concern of physicists; its effect the concern of all men"<br/>--Friedrich Dürrenmatt<br/><br/>“If one assumes a story, it must be thought through to the end”--Friedrich Dürrenmatt<br/><br/>The Physicists is a 1961 (cold war) tragic farce or black comedy by German playwright Dürrenmatt based on the 1945 bombing of Nagasaki and Hiroshima. How did the rational and supposedly apolitical discipline of science get us to the point of slaughtering millions of humans? It’s an absurd comedy, until i</span>
  <span id="freeText5181729719100482334" style="display:none">"The content of physics is the concern of physicists; its effect the concern of all men"<br />--Friedrich Dürrenmatt<br /><br />“If one assumes a story, it must be thought through to the end”--Friedrich Dürrenmatt<br /><br />The Physicists is a 1961 (cold war) tragic farce or black comedy by German playwright Dürrenmatt based on the 1945 bombing of Nagasaki and Hiroshima. How did the rational and supposedly apolitical discipline of science get us to the point of slaughtering millions of humans? It’s an absurd comedy, until it is no longer funny, of course, with a very moving conclusion, addressing the ethical dilemma that arises when politicians gain access to scientific knowledge that has the potential to destroy the world. <br /><br />Kim Jong Un and 45 will surely help us recall the relevance of this play. But it’s not just about the bomb; it’s about rapacious greed and the future of the planet.<br /><br />Three physicists (or three men who claim to be physicists) are locked up in a madhouse, Newton, Einstein, and Möbius. Sounds like the beginning of a joke? Three physicists walk into an insane asylum? Yup, it’s a grim joke, which asks about the appropriation or misappropriation or ignorance of science, but also, as does One Flew Over the Cuckoo’s Nest, who is the real madman, or who are the real madmen?<br /><br />Möbius’s final speech:<br /><br />“I am Solomon. I am poor King Solomon. Once I was immeasurably rich, wise, and God-fearing. The mighty trembled at my word. I was a Prince of Peace, a Prince of Justice. But my wisdom destroyed the fear of God. . . Now the cities over which I ruled are dead, the Kingdom that was given over into my keeping is deserted, only a blue shimmering wilderness. And somewhere round a small, yellow, nameless star there circles pointlessly, everlastingly, the radioactive earth. I am Solomon. I am Solomon. I am poor King Solomon.”<br /><br />So Möbius admits that it was mistaking scientific knowledge for wisdom that ultimately destroyed his kingdom.<br /><br />I was in a production of The Physicists at Calvin College in Fall of 1971, when I was a freshman Thespian, with the amazing Thomas Bloom as Herr Möbius. It was my first play on the big stage, and I was the Second Policeman, I think, with no lines, just standing on stage for twenty minutes and taking dead bodies off the stage. I was overwhelmed by the play, the production, moved to tears every single night. I thought this was an important message to the world, and I thought theater was a terrific vehicle for saying it. Still do.<br /> <br />You can listen to a full dramatic production, and read along with it, as I did, and here is an intro to it:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=aQYdOUQMfbI">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=aQYdO...</a><br /><br />which is followed by a thoughtful interview with Richard Rhodes who wrote a Pulitzer-Prize-winning study of the development of the bomb:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16884.The_Making_of_the_Atomic_Bomb">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1...</a><br /><br />Stanley Kubrick’s Dr. Strangelove, Or How I Learned to Stop Worrying and Love the Bomb, the phone conversation:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=dL8VIcp0ECI">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=dL8VI...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="5181729719100482334" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6838442182453065175"><b>Die Physiker;</b> eine Komodie in zwei Akten = The Physicists, c 1966, Friedrich Dürrenmatt<br/>The Physicists (German: Die Physiker) is a satiric drama written in 1961 by Swiss writer Friedrich Dürrenmatt. Informed by the Second World War and the many recent advances in science and nuclear technology, the play deals with questions of scientific ethics and humanity's ability to handle its intellectual responsibilities. It is often recognized as his most impressive yet most easily understood work.<br/><b>Characte</b></span>
  <span id="freeText6838442182453065175" style="display:none"><b>Die Physiker;</b> eine Komodie in zwei Akten = The Physicists, c 1966, Friedrich Dürrenmatt<br />The Physicists (German: Die Physiker) is a satiric drama written in 1961 by Swiss writer Friedrich Dürrenmatt. Informed by the Second World War and the many recent advances in science and nuclear technology, the play deals with questions of scientific ethics and humanity's ability to handle its intellectual responsibilities. It is often recognized as his most impressive yet most easily understood work.<br /><b>Characters:</b> Isaac Newton, Albert Einstein, Johann Wilhelm Möbius, Herbert Georg Beutler, Ernst Heinrich Ernesti, Mathilde von Zahnd, Richard Voß, Monika Stettler<br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: ششم ماه آگوست سال 1984 میلادی<br />عنوان یک: فیزیکدانها؛ اثر: فردریک دورنمات؛ برگردان: رضا کرم رضایی؛ مشخصات نشر: تهران، ابتکار، 1363، در 102 ص.، مصور، موضوع: نمایشنامه آلمانی قرن 20 م؛<br />عنوان دو: فیزیکدان ها؛ اثر:فردریک دورنمات؛ برگردان: افشین مهرسای؛ مشخصات نشر: تهران، ناردیس، چاپ نخست 1390، در 112 ص، شابک: 9786009047567؛ موضوع: نمایشنامه های آلمانی قرن 20 م<br />نمایشنامه ی فیزیکدانها (1962 میلادی) نمایشی کمدی تراژدی و مبالغه آمیز است. در تالار یک ویلا، که در زمان وقوع ماجرا خلوت است، تنها سه بیمار نگهداری میشوند، که تصادفا هر سه فیزیکدان هستند. البته نه کاملا تصادفی، آدمیزاد همیشه قوانین انساندوستی را در نظر میگیرد، و چیزهای «هم»نوع را کنار هم قرار میدهد و ... ا. شربیانی</span>

<div id="review_789784430" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/789784430" />
    <a title="Greg Brozeit" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26552534-greg-brozeit"><img alt="Greg Brozeit" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1544324486p2/26552534.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/789784430?book_show_action=true">Dec 13, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Greg Brozeit" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Greg Brozeit" href="/user/show/26552534-greg-brozeit">Greg Brozeit</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9783257208375" href="/book/show/879674.Die_Physiker">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26552534-greg-brozeit?shelf=deutsch">deutsch</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26552534-greg-brozeit?shelf=d%C3%BCrrenmatt">dürrenmatt</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26552534-greg-brozeit?shelf=drama">drama</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer789784430" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12498703620449105356">I consider <i>The Physicists</i>, together with <i>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/789786723' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Visit</a>
</i>, to be one of Friedrich Dürrenmatt’s undisputed masterpieces. A police inspector investigates the murder of nurse who has been found strangled at a sanatorium. The murderer is known, he is one of three patients at the institution who all believe themselves to be great physicists. In his case, he thinks he is Einstein. It is the second murder of a nurse in three months, the first having been committed by the patient who thinks himself to be N</span>
  <span id="freeText12498703620449105356" style="display:none">I consider <i>The Physicists</i>, together with <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/789786723" rel="nofollow noopener">The Visit</a>
</i>, to be one of Friedrich Dürrenmatt’s undisputed masterpieces. A police inspector investigates the murder of nurse who has been found strangled at a sanatorium. The murderer is known, he is one of three patients at the institution who all believe themselves to be great physicists. In his case, he thinks he is Einstein. It is the second murder of a nurse in three months, the first having been committed by the patient who thinks himself to be Newton. The third of the group, Möbius, soon after is visited by his ex-wife and their three sons—none of whom he has met before—as he is informed she has remarried a pastor and they will soon leave to do missionary work in the Marianas Islands. His visions of King Solomon will lead him toward committing a similar act as his “colleagues.”<br /><br />A further exposition of the plot would consist of one spoiler alert after the other. But for those familiar with Dürrenmatt, he revisits many of his eternal themes using the framework of his plot to expose them in thoroughly unexpected ways. He intertwines his life-long contemplation of justice, his fascination with science, and his fears and concerns about Cold War macro politics to create a small universe to amplify the social and logical inconsistencies of the real world. The questions he poses lead both to a resolution of the plot and to lingering impressions for the reader. What is insanity? Can it be sane? What is justice? Can it be self-imposed? Are the risks we take worth the ultimate prices we have to pay? Can those who shape or are responsible to uphold societal norms violate them with impunity? Who holds them accountable? What happens when those we consider to be insane are actually the most sane and vice versa?<br /><br />If those questions intrigue you, then so will <i>The Physicists</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12498703620449105356" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText4470832224810449745" style="display:none">This classic on the great ethical debate that followed the American bombings of Nagasaki and Hiroshima in 1945 is tremendous fun. <div id="review_171905464" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/171905464" />
    <a title="Vasha7" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3850379-vasha7"><img alt="Vasha7" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/171905464?book_show_action=true">May 28, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vasha7" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vasha7" href="/user/show/3850379-vasha7">Vasha7</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9783716011508" href="/book/show/148552.Die_Physiker">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer171905464" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8662443320623004497">This 1962 play is part of the wave of pessimism that followed the invention of the atomic bomb, one of the rare occasions in history that the total extinction of humanity has seemed like a real possibility. On the one hand, some thinkers stressed the essential warlike nature of humanity, as in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/183765.Robert_Ardrey' title='Robert Ardrey' rel='nofollow noopener'>Robert Ardrey</a>'s popular books about human origins as the metaphorical "children of Cain" or the famous opening sequence of <i>2001</i>. On the other hand, writers like Dürrenmatt put the blame on scientific and t</span>
  <span id="freeText8662443320623004497" style="display:none">This 1962 play is part of the wave of pessimism that followed the invention of the atomic bomb, one of the rare occasions in history that the total extinction of humanity has seemed like a real possibility. On the one hand, some thinkers stressed the essential warlike nature of humanity, as in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/183765.Robert_Ardrey" title="Robert Ardrey" rel="nofollow noopener">Robert Ardrey</a>'s popular books about human origins as the metaphorical "children of Cain" or the famous opening sequence of <i>2001</i>. On the other hand, writers like Dürrenmatt put the blame on scientific and technological progress, economic competitiveness, and general hubris, and wished for a return to simplicity or old-time religion (the last words in this play are given to King Solomon, saying, "when I no longer feared God, my wisdom destroyed my wealth"). <br /><br />Although a review like this is a poor place to expound my own views, let me just say that an atomic bomb is by no means necessary for the extinction of the human race, all that's needed is a stone axe, fire, or a large herd of goats. For the last few hundred thousand years, larger and larger areas of the planet have been turning to desert as humans cut and burn forests and graze their herds. Areas that once supported large populations no longer do; war or no war, we continue on a path that can eventually make our life impossible. Science is not a new destructive force; on the contrary, it's the one (small) chance of escape, because only now, finally, do we actually understand the large systemic causes and consequences of how we're living. We can't change unless we understand. Ignorance is only a blind rushing to destruction.<br /><br />In <i>The Physicists</i>, Dürrenmatt doesn't see it that way. He simply can't imagine any scientific progress without technological use, nor any technology that's not destructive. He has his genius physicist generate a schema of all possible applications of the ultimate theory of everything and then destroy it because it would lead to disaster. However, Dürrenmatt suggests that it's already too late to do that; Newton should never have written down his laws of gravitation in the first place. <br /><br />Dürrenmatt also somehow connects the evil in this play with the perversion of what he thinks of as "natural instincts" (i.e. domesticity, love, and children) in women -- the great manipulator is a childless, manless woman (although much is also made of her being descended from a family of warmongering profiteers) and she has other women pretend to be in love, with the intention of getting them killed. <br /><br />Anyway, although I'm glad to have read something that gave me the opportunity for so much analysis, you can probably tell that I didn't like it very much; it's baldly preachy, and though intended to be humorous, the laughter is the kind that leaves a bad taste in the mouth.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8662443320623004497" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2188654593205637029"><i>From BBC Radio 3 - Drama on 3:<br/>Samantha Bond stars as a psychiatrist in this classic European farce by Friedrich Dürrenmatt about three theoretical physicists who believe they are Einstein, Newton and Möbius. They are locked in a lunatic asylum and each gets tangled in vicious murders. Amidst all the jokes is a real relationship between a scientist who may or may not be mad and his nurse who wants to save him. The Physicists was first performed in 1962 at the height of the Cold War.<br/><br/>The serious su</i></span>
  <span id="freeText2188654593205637029" style="display:none"><i>From BBC Radio 3 - Drama on 3:<br />Samantha Bond stars as a psychiatrist in this classic European farce by Friedrich Dürrenmatt about three theoretical physicists who believe they are Einstein, Newton and Möbius. They are locked in a lunatic asylum and each gets tangled in vicious murders. Amidst all the jokes is a real relationship between a scientist who may or may not be mad and his nurse who wants to save him. The Physicists was first performed in 1962 at the height of the Cold War.<br /><br />The serious subject behind the farce is what to do with the knowledge of weapons of mass destruction once let out of the genie's bottle. Who controls that knowledge? Can scientists remain free, even in the free world?<br /><br />The music soundtrack is from Bernard Herrmann's less well known score to Fahrenheit 451.</i><br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.bbc.co.uk/programmes/b01pyfr9">http://www.bbc.co.uk/programmes/b01pyfr9</a></span>

<div id="review_253164473" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/253164473" />
    <a title="Lea (drumsofautumn)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7285286-lea-drumsofautumn"><img alt="Lea (drumsofautumn)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592339546p2/7285286.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/253164473?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lea (drumsofautumn)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lea (drumsofautumn)" href="/user/show/7285286-lea-drumsofautumn">Lea (drumsofautumn)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7285286-lea-drumsofautumn?shelf=plays-for-class">plays-for-class</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer253164473" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12210646911002071573">I've read this book in school, not expecting much of it - but i was totally surprised.<br/><br/>The story of "Die Physiker" deals with three physicists, who live in a sanatorium for the mentally ill. One of them did a groundbreaking discovery, who could influence the whole world... "Die Physiker" is a play, which teaches us a lesson about the responsibility of science.<br/><br/>I do have to mention that I am really not a fan of reading plays. You read them with such a emotionless thought, not really thinking about </span>
  <span id="freeText12210646911002071573" style="display:none">I've read this book in school, not expecting much of it - but i was totally surprised.<br /><br />The story of "Die Physiker" deals with three physicists, who live in a sanatorium for the mentally ill. One of them did a groundbreaking discovery, who could influence the whole world... "Die Physiker" is a play, which teaches us a lesson about the responsibility of science.<br /><br />I do have to mention that I am really not a fan of reading plays. You read them with such a emotionless thought, not really thinking about the setting, the look of the characters etc. Of course there is a lot to imagine by yourself then but it's just not really my thing. I like to be lead by the author and I miss this in plays. But this just is my very personal opinion, which shouldn't hold you off from reading the story.<br />Because in spite of that I really liked the book. The story captivated me from the beginning and I think it was very suspenseful. It definitely is confusing.. who is who, who is crazy, who only pretends to be crazy. But it creates a very <i>funny</i> atmosphere.<br />The ending is very surprising, in my opinion, and might be disappointing for some people. But as a whole I think the book was convincing and absolutely enjoyable. I really want to see the play one day.<br /><b>Update:</b> I saw the play in 2014 in Vienna and thought it was a little bit boring. Maybe I was too detached from the story or I just couldn't get into the way this production was done.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12210646911002071573" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1806369159617953922">Absolutely <b>terrific</b> first act, which portrays three men in an asylum, each claiming to be a brilliant physicist. It's both hilarious and macabre, as each physicist somehow winds up murdering a fawning orderly. <br/><br/>The second act, though, is something else entirely. Where the first act was macabre and absurd, the second seems intent on providing detailed explanations (which sends the delicious oddity out the window), and tailspins into a diatribe against modern technology (particularly, but not exclu</span>
  <span id="freeText1806369159617953922" style="display:none">Absolutely <b>terrific</b> first act, which portrays three men in an asylum, each claiming to be a brilliant physicist. It's both hilarious and macabre, as each physicist somehow winds up murdering a fawning orderly. <br /><br />The second act, though, is something else entirely. Where the first act was macabre and absurd, the second seems intent on providing detailed explanations (which sends the delicious oddity out the window), and tailspins into a diatribe against modern technology (particularly, but not exclusively, nuclear weapons) which is horribly dated now, but seems like it must have been heavy-handed and dull even in the original. <br /><br />Alas; I'd love to see the first act on stage, but I've no idea what I'd do with the second.</span>

<div id="review_1689534968" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1689534968" />
    <a title="Meghan Barrett" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43229537-meghan-barrett"><img alt="Meghan Barrett" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1475374868p2/43229537.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1689534968?book_show_action=true">Jul 06, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Meghan Barrett" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Meghan Barrett" href="/user/show/43229537-meghan-barrett">Meghan Barrett</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780802150882" href="/book/show/595358.Physicists">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1689534968" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15600238082450297644">Read the review here: meghan-barrett.com<br/><br/>I first reviewed this play for National Book Review Month, an awesome brainchild of SUNY Geneseo, and have expanded upon that review here. The edition of The Physicists that I read was translated by James Kirkup.<br/><br/>Friedrich Durrenmatt’s The Physicists is a delightful and sometimes silly play filled with intrigue, science, and murder, all leading up to an incredible plot twist that will leave your heart hammering and head swimming from the page-turning, gut-w</span>
  <span id="freeText15600238082450297644" style="display:none">Read the review here: meghan-barrett.com<br /><br />I first reviewed this play for National Book Review Month, an awesome brainchild of SUNY Geneseo, and have expanded upon that review here. The edition of The Physicists that I read was translated by James Kirkup.<br /><br />Friedrich Durrenmatt’s The Physicists is a delightful and sometimes silly play filled with intrigue, science, and murder, all leading up to an incredible plot twist that will leave your heart hammering and head swimming from the page-turning, gut-wrenching dialogue. Durrenmatt, a Swiss author, was heavily influence by the events of WWII when writing the play in 1961 and, in the play's more serious moments towards the end, the gravity of the events of WWII stand in the background of the play's dialogue.<br /><br />The play takes place in an insane asylum and follows three patients, all nuclear physicists. New arrivals Herbert Beutler and Ernst Ernesti believe they are Newton and Einstein respectively, while long-term resident, Johann Mobius, believes he is visited by King Solomon. After another (yes, you read another) female nurse is murdered, the authorities are at their wits ends with the madhouse; the lead psychiatrist, Dr. Mathilde von Zahnd, is forced to tighten security by bringing in new, male nurses to serve as guards. As the patients begin to get restless one night, they reveal their true selves and have a conversation that could end up changing the world…<br /><br />The play is fast paced and incredibly humorous. The plot twist at the end is masterfully executed with precise and cutting dialogue that leaves you thinking about the future of physics and the ethics of science. As an example of Durrenmatt's ability to concisely sum up an entire ethical argument, he writes:<br /><br />"NEWTON: Is it because I strangled the nurse that you want to arrest me, or because it was I who paved the way for the atomic bomb?"<br /><br />In one line, we are confronted with the huge ethical question of the role of the scientist in the outcome of their discoveries. Is Newton as complicit in the deaths of those who died via the atomic bomb (which his discoveries helped create) as he is in the death of the nurse he just directly murdered? Towards the end of the play, Durrenmatt engages with this ethical idea and more in a way that is stunning both in brevity and wit, and in the richness of the ideas' direct societal applications. Despite being over fifty years old, this play retains relevance in how it grapples with our changing scientific reality.<br /><br />Overall, this is masterful play with well-balanced satire; The Physicists is accessible to every audience regardless of someone’s familiarity with physics.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15600238082450297644" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_42437462" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/42437462" />
    <a title="Rochelle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1873383-rochelle"><img alt="Rochelle" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rochelle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rochelle" href="/user/show/1873383-rochelle">Rochelle</a>
      </span>

          added it

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer42437462" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15833267748059682309">The Physicists by Friedrich Durrenmatt Reading Notes 1/09<br/><br/>Setting: The drama is set in a decaying villa that is part of a modern sanitarium in a small town in Germany. The author spends many words with exacting details of his setting including the color—white –of the furniture, the washable, glossy walls, and the fact that there is a prison nearby. He is attempting to adhere to the Aristotelian unities of time place and action. <br/>If I were to produce this play, I would have to closely examine the s</span>
  <span id="freeText15833267748059682309" style="display:none">The Physicists by Friedrich Durrenmatt Reading Notes 1/09<br /><br />Setting: The drama is set in a decaying villa that is part of a modern sanitarium in a small town in Germany. The author spends many words with exacting details of his setting including the color—white –of the furniture, the washable, glossy walls, and the fact that there is a prison nearby. He is attempting to adhere to the Aristotelian unities of time place and action. <br />If I were to produce this play, I would have to closely examine the symbolic meanings of all the props to decide how closely I would adhere to the written setting. <br /><br />Synopsis of the plot: Three famous and elderly physicists are housed in the oldest section of a mental institution for the wealthy, Les Cerisiers, (The Cherry Trees) There they live together fairly isolated from family, society, and the rest of the sanatorium. Each man believes he is someone else, Newton, Einstein, and Mobius (German, 1858, Mobius strip mathematician). Moreover, Mobius believes he is in a direct conversational relationship with King Solomon who guides his actions. Sometimes the inmates also believe themselves to be other scientists.(Kilton: the theory of Equivalence, Eisler?) Each is attended by a private nurse described as very physically fit woman with whom they develop what in their eyes is an untenable relationship so they kill them. The men are defended by the doctor of the asylum, a hunchbacked, aristocratic Dr Mathilde vov Zahnd, as being assailants and not responsible for their actions. The local police make only one demand---that only male attendants are hired, and this is accomplished. As time goes on, the head doctor, adopts for herself the idea of communicating with King Solomon and she goes insane with the implication that she leads an not only an institution for the insane, but an insane world. The play closes with monologues by each of the patients as their alter-character, reporting, bemoaning, or accepting responsibility, for their actions-----<br />Newton: unresolved problems concerning the nature of gravitational force.<br />Einstein: his suggestion that the atomic bomb be developed relying on E=mc2.<br />Mobius: as Solomon: allowing wisdom to destroy his fear of GOD, thus losing wealth and seeing a radioactive earth circling a nameless star (? the sun). <br /> <br /> <br />A Möbius strip made with a piece of paper and tape. If an ant were to crawl along the length of this strip, it would return to its starting point without ever crossing an edge, having traversed the entire length of the strip. Wiki<br /><br />Points to ponder:<br /> The Cherry trees----a reference to virginity ?of the nurses ?as the ultimate state?<br /><br /><br />Newton: on order---he became a physicist to “interpret the apparent disorder o f Nature in the light of a more sublime order” p.18<br /><br /><br />Mobius (on the appearance of King Solomon) “…in the realm of science, the is nothing more repugnant than a miracle. P.48<br /><br /><br />The Inspector: What is Justice? p.65 Justice is Justice<br /><br />???Are all new ideas insanities??? What is the connection between science and moral responsibility???<br /><br />Newton: “ …you are a genius and therefore common property” p.74<br /><br />Mobius: On the responsibility of the scientist to be aware of the results of his calculations…” New and inconsivable forces would be unleshed, making possible a technical advance….were to fall into the mands of mankind.<br /><br /> Doktor: Everything that can be thought is thought at some time or another. Now or in the future. P.89<br /><br /><br />,</span>
  <a data-text-id="15833267748059682309" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1995186416" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1995186416" />
    <a title="Missy J" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7406091-missy-j"><img alt="Missy J" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1481102743p2/7406091.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1995186416?book_show_action=true">May 09, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Missy J" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Missy J" href="/user/show/7406091-missy-j">Missy J</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7406091-missy-j?shelf=2017-books">2017-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7406091-missy-j?shelf=europe-related">europe-related</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7406091-missy-j?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7406091-missy-j?shelf=read-in-german">read-in-german</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7406091-missy-j?shelf=switzerland-related">switzerland-related</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7406091-missy-j?shelf=play">play</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1995186416" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8660156032980182911">A bizarre short play by the Swiss author Friedrich Duerrenmatt. "The Physicists" is considered a German-language classic, posing ethical questions on man's responsibility of its scientific inventions and how technology can be used against humanity.<br/><br/>The play takes places in a sanitorium. There are three patients there who consider themselves to be Newton, Einstein and King Solomon respectively, and are thus regarded as madmen. A police inspector is visiting the medical facility for a second time a</span>
  <span id="freeText8660156032980182911" style="display:none">A bizarre short play by the Swiss author Friedrich Duerrenmatt. "The Physicists" is considered a German-language classic, posing ethical questions on man's responsibility of its scientific inventions and how technology can be used against humanity.<br /><br />The play takes places in a sanitorium. There are three patients there who consider themselves to be Newton, Einstein and King Solomon respectively, and are thus regarded as madmen. A police inspector is visiting the medical facility for a second time already, after a second nurse has been strangled by one of these 3 patients. <br /><br />At first, I thought this would be a murder mystery, but more than halfway through the play, Duerrenmatt suddenly presents the audience with the morale message of the play. How increased scientific knowledge can be used against humanity. How man isn't prepared for the scientific findings he has discovered. The ending took another unexpected U-turn. Overall, a very bizarre play.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  <i>Die Vernunft forderte diesen Schritt. Wir sind in unserer Wissenschaft an die Grenzen des Erkennbaren gestossen. Wir wissen einige genau erfassbare Gesetze, einige Grundbeziehungen zwischen unbegreiflichen Erscheinungen, das ist alles, der gewaltige Rest bleibt Geheimnis, dem Verstaende unzugaenglich. Wir haben das Ende unseres Weges erreicht. Aber die Menschheit ist noch nicht soweit. Wir haben uns vorgekaempft, nun folgt uns niemand nach, wir sind in Leere gestossen. Unsere Wissenschaft ist schrecklich geworden, unsere Forschung gefaehrlich, unsere Erkenntnis toedlich.<br /><br />Nur Im Irrenhaus sind wir noch frei. Nur im Irrenhaus duerfen wir noch denken. In der Freiheit sind unsere Gedanken Sprengstoff. <br /><br />Entweder bleiben wir im Irrenhaus, oder die Welt wird eines. Entweder loeschen wir uns im Gedaechtnis der Menschen aus, oder die Menschheit erlischt. <br /></i>
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>
  <a data-text-id="8660156032980182911" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

Amidst all the jokes is a real relationship between a scientist who may or may not be mad and his nurse who wants to save him. The Physicists was first performed in 1962 at the height of the Cold War.<br><br>The serious subject behind the farce is what to do with the knowledge of weapons of mass destruction once let out of the genie's bottle. Who controls that knowledge? Can scientists remain free, even in the free world?<br><br>The music soundtrack is from Bernard Herrmann's less well known score to <a href="http://youtu.be/lmKDQvnrOdk" rel="nofollow noopener">Fahrenheit 451</a>.</i><br><br>CAST LIST<br>Doctor Von Zahnd ..... <div id="review_43366969" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/43366969" />
    <a title="Brian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1260617-brian"><img alt="Brian" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/43366969?book_show_action=true">Jan 17, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brian" href="/user/show/1260617-brian">Brian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780802150882" href="/book/show/595358.Physicists">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1260617-brian?shelf=2009">2009</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1260617-brian?shelf=drama">drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1260617-brian?shelf=switzerland">switzerland</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1260617-brian?shelf=1960s">1960s</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer43366969" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15964459892847656968">Three physicists are locked away in a sanatarium run by the the world-renowned psychiatrist Mathilde van Zahndt, and the body count is rising. Inmates believing themselves to be Albert Einstein and Sir Isaac Newton have each already killed a nurse, and as the first scene closes Johann Wilhelm Mobius kills a nurse (on the orders of King Solomon)who professes her love to him and wants to establish a new life away from the sanatarium. But in the second scene, it is revealed that all is not as it se</span>
  <span id="freeText15964459892847656968" style="display:none">Three physicists are locked away in a sanatarium run by the the world-renowned psychiatrist Mathilde van Zahndt, and the body count is rising. Inmates believing themselves to be Albert Einstein and Sir Isaac Newton have each already killed a nurse, and as the first scene closes Johann Wilhelm Mobius kills a nurse (on the orders of King Solomon)who professes her love to him and wants to establish a new life away from the sanatarium. But in the second scene, it is revealed that all is not as it seems - for the three physicists or the psychiatrist.<br /><br />Friedrich Durrenmatt, considered one of the world's leading German-language playwrights, uses the situation to tackle seemingly contradictory concepts - the freedom of incarceration (and the imprisonment of the free), the sanity of the insane (and the insanity of the sane) - as well as discuss the moral obligations of both scientists and society. The play engages the reader both for its philosophical observations and its absurdist plot. <br /><br />Madcap, intellectual, and absurd, Durrenmatt's The Physicists is well-worth a read, even if it won't take more than a hour to finish it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15964459892847656968" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1449194100" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1449194100" />
    <a title="Jill" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6638317-jill"><img alt="Jill" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1368732701p2/6638317.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1449194100?book_show_action=true">Nov 22, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jill" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jill" href="/user/show/6638317-jill">Jill</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" 9780394172460" href="/book/show/1990794.The_Physicists">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jill by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/22493260-brandon-wicke">Brandon Wicke</a>
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1449194100" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13360895032512301671">Our genius is not our own. Everything we say aloud, to an audience, is subject to interpretation, to future context, to the possibility of contributing to something we never intended. Writers or scientists, words are dangerous.<br/><br/>Sharply and smartly, this play explores that idea, coupling it with the unreliability of madness. Do the ideas stand alone; do we have <i>any</i> ultimate control -- not only over what's done with what we say, but over ourselves, proper?<br/><br/>Intellectual as it is, this is no closet dr</span>
  <span id="freeText13360895032512301671" style="display:none">Our genius is not our own. Everything we say aloud, to an audience, is subject to interpretation, to future context, to the possibility of contributing to something we never intended. Writers or scientists, words are dangerous.<br /><br />Sharply and smartly, this play explores that idea, coupling it with the unreliability of madness. Do the ideas stand alone; do we have <i>any</i> ultimate control -- not only over what's done with what we say, but over ourselves, proper?<br /><br />Intellectual as it is, this is no closet drama --- definitely meant to be performed, it's a great read nonetheless. Really this is a 3.5, and I imagine seeing it live would bump it much higher.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13360895032512301671" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('xdTFjuE+vzMM93lQVdKZoT4NVYqcRJ57LTP3fCbyfwlClfWsWuq1XmeJ7sRfin1lh3v0+a/DsUa1wfdGOTIZiA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Pba5+ZxUV1x4H6OCPz7x12kx3Swt+6hfsNslZMF0DxW694nbJ4BdMRNhNBY1ZhUT0Ed8Xx58h2IoKSVe3rRplA==')})
  }

Der Text, der sich mit seiner Bezeichnung als &quot;dramatisches Gedicht&quot; der Festlegung auf eine der dramatischen Gattungen entzieht, trug wesentlich dazu bei, den Blankvers als den klassischen deutschen Dramenvers zu etablieren. Mit seinem Nathan reagierte Lessing auf die religiöse Orthodoxie und Intoleranz seiner Zeit. Ort der Handlung ist Jerusalem während der Kreuzzüge – eine Stadt, in der Christentum, Islam und Judentum direkt aufeinandertreffen. Höhepunkt des Stücks, in dem es um eine moral- und geschichtsphilosophische Botschaft, um die Aufforderung zu Toleranz und Humanität geht, ist die berühmte Ringparabel, die der reiche jüdische Kaufmann Nathan erzählt: Sie soll die hintergründige Frage des Sultans Saladin beantworten, welche der drei Religionen die wahre sei. Jahrhundert - eine literarische Form, die mit der klassizistischen Auffassung und Tragödie bricht und Probleme des häuslich-privaten Bereichs und den Konflikt der Stände zum Gegenstand der tragischen Handlung macht. In sehr direkter Weise wird in Schillers \'Kabale und Liebe\' mit seinem auf Spannung angelegten klaren Aufbau Kritik an den politischen Zuständen in der Fürstentümern geübt: Willkür, Mätressenwirtschaft, Verkauf von Landeskindern. Das bürgerliche Trauerspiel kontrastiert die verrottete Moral des Hofes mit der Tugend des Bürgertums. Im Mittelpunkt des Sturm-und-Drang-Stücks steht die tragische Liebesbeziehung zwischen Angehörigen verschiedener Stände: zwischen dem Adligen Ferdinand und der Musikertochter Luise Miller. \'Kabale       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/41616.Friedrich_D_rrenmatt">About Friedrich Dürrenmatt</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/41616.Friedrich_D_rrenmatt"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1201465055p3/41616.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/41616.Friedrich_D_rrenmatt">Friedrich Dürrenmatt

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer2867622399839931295">Friedrich Dürrenmatt (1921 – 1990) was a Swiss author and dramatist.<br/><br/>Dürrenmatt was born in the Emmental (canton of Bern), the son of a Protestant pastor. His grandfather Ulrich Dürrenmatt was a conservative politician. The family moved to Bern in 1935. Dürrenmatt began to study philosophy and German language and literature at the University of Zurich in 1941, but moved to the University of Bern af</span>
  <span id="freeText2867622399839931295" style="display:none">Friedrich Dürrenmatt (1921 – 1990) was a Swiss author and dramatist.<br /><br />Dürrenmatt was born in the Emmental (canton of Bern), the son of a Protestant pastor. His grandfather Ulrich Dürrenmatt was a conservative politician. The family moved to Bern in 1935. Dürrenmatt began to study philosophy and German language and literature at the University of Zurich in 1941, but moved to the University of Bern after one semester. In 1943 he decided to become an author and dramatist and dropped his academic career. In 1945-46, he wrote his first play, "It is written". On October 11 1946 he married actress Lotti Geissler. She died in 1983 and Dürrenmatt was married again to another actress, Charlotte Kerr, the following year.<br /><br />He was a proponent of epic theater whose plays reflected the recent experiences of World War II. The politically active author gained fame largely due to his avant-garde dramas, philosophically deep crime novels, and often macabre satire. One of his leading sentences was: "A story is not finished, until it has taken the worst turn". Dürrenmatt was a member of the Gruppe Olten.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2867622399839931295" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
